77 (686)
Indra
Kurusuti KƒÅ·πáva
11 verses: gƒÅyatrƒ´ arranged in trcas 1‚Äì9; br Ã• hat Ã• ƒ´ 10, satobrÃ•hatƒ´ 11, arranged in a pragƒÅtha
This hymn primarily treats the obscure Emu·π£a myth, a boyhood deed of Indra‚Äôs. As far as can be determined, in the RÃ•gvedic version (there are later Vedic prose ver sions, e.g., Taittirƒ´ya Sa·πÉhitƒÅ VI.2.4.2‚Äì3) the just-born Indra, after a brief dialogue with his mother, takes a bow named Bunda and kills a boar named Emu·π£a, allow ing him to capture (or Vi·π£·πáu to capture for him) a special mess of rice porridge, as well as some buffalo, which he cooks for his father and mother. The most impor tant (and essentially only) RÃ•gvedic passages about this myth outside of VIII.77 are I.61.7, VIII.45.4‚Äì5, VIII.69.14‚Äì15, and VIII.96.2.
Our hymn begins with Indra‚Äôs dialogue with his mother (vss. 1‚Äì2), a dialogue found in the same words in VIII.45.4‚Äì5. Since in that passage Indra takes the Bunda bow prior to questioning his mother, we can connect the first two verses of this hymn with the story in verses 6‚Äì8, even though the Bunda bow is not men
tioned here in verses 1‚Äì2 and other material intervenes. Between the dialogue of verses 1‚Äì2 and the rest of the story starting in verse 6 are several references to other deeds of Indra‚Äôs, at least one of them (vs. 5) even more obscure than the Emu·π£a myth. It does seem likely, however, that the drinking of prodigious amounts of soma in verse 4 is part of the Emu·π£a story: in III.48, also about Indra‚Äôs boyhood and his relationship to mother and father, he consumes vast amounts of soma right after birth.
When the story is taken up again, we first are given a general summary of the myth in 6ab, and then the first incident in the story, the taking of the Bunda bow, in 6c. In our opinion, verse 8 is the speech of Indra‚Äôs mother, urging him to cap ture the porridge by means of the bow. Her ‚Äújust born right now‚Äù (vs. 8c) echoes the ‚Äújust born‚Äù that begins verse 1, and these two phrases form a ring defining the Emu·π£a story. Verse 7 continues the story of the Bunda bow and the aiming of the arrow. Verse 9, the final verse in the gƒÅyatrƒ´ meter, is a standard summary of Indra‚Äôs great deeds.
The last two verses (10‚Äì11) are in different meter. The first gives a pr√©cis of the myth, introducing Vi·π£·πáu‚Äôs role in securing the rice porridge, a detail that does not appear in accord with verse 6. The final pƒÅda of verse 10 identifies Indra‚Äôs opponent as a boar (no mention of the boar in the earlier verses) and in its final word provides the boar‚Äôs name, Emu·π£a, as the solution of the riddle. (The name Emu·π£a occurs only here in the RÃ•gveda.) The version given in verse 10 agrees better with the one in I.61.7 than it does with the treatment in the rest of this hymn and indeed in the rest of Ma·πá·∏çala VIII, which contains the other pertinent passages, and one wonders if the poet was trying to harmonize two versions that he knew of this myth by tack
ing the Vi·π£·πáu verse onto the Vi·π£·πáu-less version found in the gƒÅyatrƒ´ portion of the hymn. The final verse (11) is a high rhetorical celebration of the Bunda bow and of Indra‚Äôs arms that wield it.

‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡§§‡§∞‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ç

Barely born, he of hundred powers
Began to question his mother thus:
Who are these fierce ones? Indeed, who are notorious?

Jamison Brereton Notes

The hapax pratidhƒÅÃÅ- is connected by Grassmann to ‚àödhƒÅ ‚Äòsuck(le)‚Äô, presumably because of the soma-drinking context. But I take it rather to ‚àödhƒÅ ‚Äòplace‚Äô (so also Scarlatta, though with different semantics). The lexeme pr√°ti ‚àödhƒÅ means ‚Äòset (an arrow on a bow), aim‚Äô, and from ‚Äòaim‚Äô to ‚Äòshot‚Äô seems an easy semantic path. Consider also English ‚Äòshot‚Äô for a quickly downed drink. That a bow and arrows figure in the Emu·π£a myth (see vss. 6-7) may support this derivation.

The other hapax in this verse, kƒÅ·πáukƒÅÃÅis not so easily handled. All tr. and comm. (including MM, EWA, s.v.) essentially give up. Grassmann calls it a ‚ÄúBeiwort zu s√°ras-‚Äù (the ponds in pƒÅda b) and takes it as an acc. pl. neut., which seems reasonable, though it could also in principle be an old instr. sg. of an -a- or -ƒÅ-stem (as pratidhƒÅÃÅ is). Unlike some other passages containing opaque hapaxes, there is no phonological motivation visible. It resembles the hapax participle ka·πá≈´kay√°nt- in X.132.7, but that is of no help because that form is even more puzzling than this one. It also resembles kƒÅ·πá√°- ‚Äòone-eyed (RV X.155.1, AV+), and Edgerton (1911, The -k-suffixes of Indo- Iranian, 13) suggests it is derived from that word and means ‘jug’ ‚Äî a jug ‚Äúwith a small opening and a large bulging body,‚Äù though he’s not happy about the -u-. One contributor to the word‚Äôs appearing here might be a pun on the v·πõddhi derivative kƒÅ·πáv√°- ‚Äòdescendant of Ka·πáva‚Äô, which is several times read as kƒÅ·πáu v√°- (VIII.2.40, 4.20); the Anukrama·πáƒ´ attributes this hymn to Kurusuti KƒÅ·πáva.

Jamison Brereton Notes

I have no idea who or what the Gandharva represents here. Geldner suggests that it can be the Gandharva as Somabewacher or, alternatively, the sun, but the context gives no particular support to either identification. It would be desirable to connect this verse with the Emu·π£a myth, but I do not see how to do that either. It should be noted, however, that the datival phrase brahm√°bhya·∏• ‚Ä¶ v·πõdh√© ‚Äúto strengthen the composers of sacred formulations‚Äù is reminiscent of the datival phrase in vs. 8, an undoubted Emu·π£a verse, stot·πõÃÅbhya·∏• ‚Ä¶ n·πõÃÅbhyo nƒÅÃÅribhyo √°ttave ‚Äúfor the praisers, the superior men and their ladies, to eat.‚Äù Note in our phrase the ‚Äúattraction‚Äù of the obj. brahm√°bhya·∏• into the dat. to match the inf. v·πõdh√©. Unless we wish to interpr. the phrase as passive, ‚Äúfor the composers ‚Ä¶ to be strengthened.‚Äù On the lexeme abh√≠ ‚àöt·πõd, see comm. ad IX.110.5. It is generally specialized for the drilling into the cave in the Vala myth, but that seems to give us no help here.

The one clear connection to the Emu·π£a myth within the hymn is the adj.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ü‡§¶‡•ç+‡§à‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡§¶‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞ ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§§‡§æ (‡§∂‡§µ‡§∏‡•Ä) ‡§µ‡§π ‡§¨‡§≤‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§≠‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç) ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á (‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§π‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç ! (‡§î‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§µ‡§æ‡§≠‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§â‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§®‡§æ‡§≠ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§´‡•à‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ö‡§π‡•Ä‡§∂‡•Å‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§ü‡§ø‡§≤‡§ó‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§¶‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∂‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§á‡§®‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§Ö‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§ú‡§ó‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•Ä ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§§ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§π‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ! ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§æ) ‡§®‡§ø‡§ñ‡§ø‡§≤ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ò‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡§ï ‡§µ‡§π ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ (‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§â‡§® ‡§ö‡•ã‡§∞ ‡§°‡§æ‡§ï‡•Ç ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∂‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç+‡§Ö‡§ñ‡§ø‡§¶‡§§‡•ç) ‡§∞‡§ó‡•ú ‡§°‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§â‡§®‡•ç‡§π‡•á‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤ ‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§¶‡•á‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§¶‡•á‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç (‡§á‡§µ) ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡•á (‡§ñ‡•á) ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§õ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§ñ‡§ï‡§∞ (‡§ñ‡•á‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•á (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) ‡§õ‡•ã‡§ü‡•á-‡§õ‡•ã‡§ü‡•á ‡§°‡§Ç‡§°‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∞‡§ó‡§°‡§º‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ (‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§π‡§æ) ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§ö‡•ã‡§∞, ‡§°‡§æ‡§ï‡•Ç, ‡§Ü‡§§‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¶‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§¶‡•á‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§™‡§• ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§µ‡§π‡•Ä (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§É) ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡•ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞ ‡§â‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§§ (‡§Ö‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡•©‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThat single shaft of yours, Indra, which you make your ally, is hundred-pointed, thousand-feathered.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Your arrow with a hundred ruddy glints and a thousand feathers was the single one
that you made into your yokemate, o Indra.

Jamison Brereton Notes

bradhn√°- is otherwise a color term (‚Äòcoppery, ruddy‚Äô) or a substance characterized by that color (soma, in nearby VIII.69.7), but in this hapax bahuvrƒ´hi, ≈õat√°-bradhna- it must refer to something capable of being counted, hence my ‚Äòruddy glints‚Äô. Grassmann‚Äôs ‚Äòhundert Metallspitzen habend‚Äô, Geldner‚Äôs ‚Äòmit hundert Spitzen‚Äô seem to stray too far from the color term. The appearance of this word here may be owing to its phonological similarity to bund√°-.

Griffith

One only is that shaft of thine, with thousand feathers, hundred barbs,
Which, Indra, thou hast made thy friend.

Dein Pfeil mit hundert Spitzen, mit tausend Federn war der Einzige, den du, Indra, zu deinem Verb√ºndeten gemacht hast.

An hundert Spitzen hat dein Pfeil, und tausend Federn er allein, Den Indra du dir zugesellt.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As indicated in the published introduction, I take this verse as the speech of Indra‚Äôs mother, picking up from the dialogue in vss. 1-2 that began (1a) jaj√±ƒÅn√≥ n√∫ ‚Äújust born,‚Äù which is echoed here by sady√≥ jƒÅt√°·∏• (‚Äújust born right now‚Äù). In VIII.45.4 Indra took the Bunda bow just before his dialogue with his mother, and in VIII.69.14-15 he’s a tiny child when he gets the porridge and cooks the buffalo for his mother and father.

I supply odan√°m ‚Äòrice porridge‚Äô as the object of ƒÅÃÅbhara (and √°ttave). Note that in the summary verse 10 various foodstuffs, including odan√°m, are objects of ƒÅÃÅbharat.

The inclusion of ‚Äòladies‚Äô (nƒÅÃÅribhya·∏•) alongside superior men (n·πõÃ•bhya·∏•) is quite rare in the RV. See I.43.6, where it seems to refer to humankind in general.

Here it seems to have a more restricted sense and could actually refer to the gods and their wives: n·πõÃÅ- is often used of divinities. For goddesses beside gods see nearby VIII.80.10, though it is attributed to a different poet.

The voc. ·πõbhu·π£·π≠hira is a little surprising. Both Grassmann and Geldner take it as implicitly comparative: Grassmann ‚Äòstark, t√ºchtig wie ·πöbhu‚Äô, Geldner ‚Äò‚Ä¶ stark wie die ·πöbhu‚Äôs‚Äô. I take it rather as parallel to ·πõbhuk·π£√°n- ‚Äòmaster of the ·πöbhus‚Äô, which is regularly an epithet of Indra.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThe wide-traversing Sun, despatched by you, brings (to the world) all these (waters which you create);he brings hundreds of cattle and rice boiled in milk; it is Indra who slays the water-stealing boar.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

The sun (herecalled Vi·π£·πáu), as the bringer of rain, is said to bring the cattle and food which the rain produces; the ‘boar’varƒÅha is a person nification of the cloud as smitten by Indra’s thunderbolt (cf. Nirukta 5.4). Taittirƒ´ya Sa·πÉhitƒÅ6.2.4 relates the legend: the person nified sacrifice concealed itself from the gods, and assuming the form ofVi·π£·πáu, entered the earth. The gods, streching out their hands, sought in vain to lay hold of it; but wherever itturned, Indra, outstripping it, stood in front of it. It said to him, ‘Who is this that, outstripping me, always stands infront of me?’ He answered, “I slay in inaccessible plural ces’. Then, it said to him, ‘You say that you can slay ininaccessible plural ces– if this be so, the boar vƒÅmamo·π£a (vƒÅmamu·π£a in Caraka BrƒÅhma·πáa) guards for theasuras, behind the seven mountains, the wealth which the gods must obtain; prove your title by slaying thatboar’. Indra, seizing up a tuft of darbha grass, pierced those mountains and slew him. Then he said to thesacrifice, ‘You said that you could bring out from inaccessible plural ces; bring him out from there’. It brought out allthe instrumental uments of the sacrifice (i.e. the altar, Soma jars, cup·π£..) and gave them to the gods’. The CarakaBrƒÅhma·πáa adds that the boar hid behind twenty-one stone cities. The seven mountains are the four dƒ´k·π£ƒÅsor initiatory rites and the three upasads; the boar vƒÅmamo·π£a (stealer of precious things) is the person nifiedceremony of pressing the Soma; see also RV 1.61.7

Jamison Brereton

All these things Vi·π£·πáu brought here, the wide-striding one spurred on by you:
a hundred buffaloes, a rice porridge cooked in milk‚Äî(when) Indra
(pierced) the boar Emu·π£a.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Oldenberg and Geldner (see his n. 10d) read loc. √≠ndre contra the Pp. nom. √≠ndra·∏• and interpret the verse as meaning that Vi·π£·πáu brought Emu·π£a to Indra, along with the food named in pƒÅda c. But this seems unlikely: the central fact of the myth is that Indra pierced the boar. Cf. I.61.7d v√≠dhyad varƒÅh√°·πÉ tir√≥ √°drim √°stƒÅ ‚ÄúHe pierced the boar through the stone, (Indra) the archer.‚Äù This last pƒÅda contains both Indra and the boar and ends with the boar‚Äôs name as if as the solution of a riddle (remember that this is the only occurrence of the name in the RV), and it would seem rhetorically anticlimactic for the image merely to be one of Vi·π£·πáu lugging the corpse of the boar, along with some food, to Indra. Instead it seems the encapsulation of the myth, and I would further argue that it finally closes the incomplete syntactic circle begun in verse 6. In 6a we find n√≠r ƒÅvidhyad gir√≠bhya ƒÅÃÅ‚Äú(he) pierced from/to/through the mountains,‚Äù with the signature verb and the signature locale (see √°drim in I.61.7 just quoted), but neither subject nor object. But in 10d we have both subject (reading nom. √≠ndra·∏• with the Pp.) and object, but no verb. This seems a clever variant on ring composition, where a structure begun at the beginning of a section is completed at the end. Even if, as I suggest in the introduction, vs. 10 is a tacked-on addition to the original Vi·π£·πáu-less version of the myth found in the rest of this hymn (and other mentions in VIII), the poet has cleverly made use of the underdefined treatment in 6a to attach vs. 10 more closely to vss. 6-9 by supplying the missing pieces in 10d.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúFar-darting is your well-made auspicious bow, unfailing is your golden arrow; your two warlike arms areready equipped, destructively overthrowing, destructively piercing.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

YƒÅska Nirukta 6.33: ·πõd≈´pe cid·πõd≈´v·πõdhƒÅ; like two bees delighting in sweetness;

·πödu = m·πõdu; madhu, Soma

Jamison Brereton

Powerfully ruling, well-made, made of boar is your bow, the golden Bunda, that aims straight.
Your two arms are fit for battle, well-equipped, increasing injury even for one who protects himself from injury.

Jamison Brereton Notes

s≈´m√°ya- is generally analyzed as having the adverbial prefix su- ‚Äògood‚Äô, hence ‚Äòaus gutem Stoff‚Äô (Geldner). However, AiG II.2.770 reports a suggestion of Thieme’s (apparently only reported in AiG, not published) that it’s ‚Äòaus Eber(-zahn) verfertigt‚Äô, with an unextended root noun s≈´- ‚Äò(wild) pig, boar‚Äô, which is the basis of s≈´kar√°- ‚Äòboar‚Äô (already RV VII.55.4) and which has numerous IE cognates.

Although Mayrhofer explicitly rejects this suggestion (KEWA, s.v. s≈´kar√°-; EWA, s.vv. MAY1 , s≈´kar√°-), context favors Thieme‚Äôs suggestion. The boar Emu·π£a was clearly a formidable opponent of Indra‚Äôs, and the use of a weapon made from the same animal to defeat it makes good sense from the point of view of sympathetic magic. Consider also the association of boars with ritual of kingship, and note, in particular, that using parts of the boar for the armor and weaponry of warriors is found elsewhere (cf. Odysseus‚Äôs boar‚Äôs tusk helmet and the general disc. in Jamison 1999 [Penelope and the Pigs], ClAnt. 18: 258-70). The unextended root noun s≈´could be preserved in this old myth, esp. since it could be assimilated to the prefix su-.

My interpretation of pƒÅda d essentially follows Scarlatta (301, 516); see also EWA, s.v. ARD.

Jamison Brereton Notes

On these verses as possible explanatory additions to the Emu·π£a version given earlier in the hymn, see published introduction.

Griffith

Most deadly is thy bow, successful, fashioned well: good is thine arrow, decked with gold.
Warlike and well equipped thine arms are, which increase sweetness for him who drinks the sweet.

Geldner

Viele verwundend, wohlgemacht, aus gutem Stoff ist dein Bogen, der treffliche, goldene Bunda. Deine beiden Arme sind kampflustig, wohlgeformt, die verwundbaren Stellen treffend auch bei dem, der sie sch√ºtzt.

Grassmann

Gut ist dein Bogen, viele tilgend, sch√∂nbereitet, gut treffend ist dein goldner Pfeil; erfreulich und sch√∂n gef√ºllt sind deine Arme, und S√ºssigkeit liebt dein Lippenpaar.
